<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Slavery in the United States</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For slavery among Native Americans, see <a href="/wiki/Slavery_among_Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery among Native Americans in the United States">Slavery among Native Americans in the United States</a>. For slavery in the colonial period, see <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_colonial_United_States" title="Slavery in the colonial United States">Slavery in the colonial United States</a>. For modern-day slavery, see <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking_in_the_United_States" title="Human trafficking in the United States">Human trafficking in the United States</a>.</div>

<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:African_American_topics_sidebar" title="Template:African American topics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:African_American_topics_sidebar" title="Template talk:African American topics sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:African_American_topics_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>

<p><b>Slavery in the United States</b> was the legal institution of human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chattel_slavery" title="Chattel slavery">chattel enslavement</a>, primarily of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Africans" title="Africans">Africans</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a>, that existed in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States of America</a> in the 18th and 19th centuries after it gained independence and before the end of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. Slavery had been practiced in <a href="/wiki/British_America" title="British America">British America</a> from early colonial days, and was legal in all <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a> at the time of the Declaration of Independence in 1776.</p>
<p>By the time of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> (1775–1783), the status of slave had been institutionalized as a racial caste associated with African ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> When the United States Constitution was ratified (1789), a relatively small number of <a href="/wiki/Free_people_of_color" title="Free people of color">free people of color</a> were among the voting citizens (male property owners).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> During and immediately following the Revolutionary War, <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolitionist</a> laws were passed in most <a href="/wiki/Northern_United_States" title="Northern United States">Northern states</a> and a movement developed to abolish slavery. Most of these states had a higher proportion of free labor than in the South and economies based on different industries. They abolished slavery by the end of the 18th century, some with gradual systems that kept adults as slaves for two decades. But the rapid expansion of the <a href="/wiki/King_Cotton" title="King Cotton">cotton industry</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a> after the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Cotton_gin" title="Cotton gin">cotton gin</a> greatly increased demand for slave labor, and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern states</a> continued as slave societies. Those states attempted to extend slavery into the new Western territories to keep their share of political power in the nation; Southern leaders also wanted to annex <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> to be used as a slave territory. The United States became polarized over the issue of slavery, represented by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_and_free_states" title="Slave and free states">slave and free states</a> divided by the <a href="/wiki/Mason%E2%80%93Dixon_line" title="Mason–Dixon line">Mason–Dixon line</a>, which separated free <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a> from slave <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>.</p>
<p>Congress during the <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Jefferson</a> administration <a href="/wiki/Act_Prohibiting_Importation_of_Slaves" title="Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves">prohibited</a> the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">importation of slaves</a>, effective 1808, although smuggling (illegal importing) was not unusual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Julia_Floyd_Smith_1973.2C_pp._44-46_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Julia_Floyd_Smith_1973.2C_pp._44-46-3">[3]</a></sup> Domestic slave trading, however, continued at a rapid pace, driven by labor demands from the development of cotton <a href="/wiki/Plantations_in_the_American_South" title="Plantations in the American South">plantations</a> in the Deep South. More than one million slaves were sold from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_South" title="Upper South">Upper South</a>, which had a surplus of labor, and taken to the Deep South in a forced migration, splitting up many families. New communities of African-American culture were developed in the Deep South, and the total slave population in the South eventually reached 4 million before liberation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stephen1999_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stephen1999-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the West was developed for settlement, the Southern state governments wanted to keep a balance between the number of slave and free states to maintain a political balance of power in <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a>. The new <a href="/wiki/Territories_of_the_United_States" title="Territories of the United States">territories</a> acquired from <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">France</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> were the subject of major political compromises. By 1850, the newly rich cotton-growing South was threatening to secede from the <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a>, and tensions continued to rise. Many white Southern Christians, including church ministers, attempted to justify their support for slavery<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> as modified by Christian paternalism. The largest denominations, the Baptist, Methodist, and Presbyterian churches, split over the slavery issue into regional organizations of the North and South. When <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> won the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1860" title="United States presidential election, 1860">1860 election</a> on a platform of halting the expansion of slavery, seven states broke away to form the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy</a>. The first six states to secede held the greatest number of slaves in the South. Shortly after, the Civil War began when Confederate forces attacked the US Army's Fort Sumter. Four additional slave states then seceded. Due to Union measures such as the <a href="/wiki/Confiscation_Acts" title="Confiscation Acts">Confiscation Acts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a> in 1863, the war effectively ended slavery, even before ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Thirteenth Amendment</a> in December 1865 formally ended the legal institution throughout the United States.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_America">Colonial America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Colonial America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_colonial_United_States" title="Slavery in the colonial United States">Slavery in the colonial United States</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Indian_slave_trade_in_the_American_Southeast" title="Indian slave trade in the American Southeast">Indian slave trade in the American Southeast</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_enslavement_of_indigenous_peoples_in_California" title="History of enslavement of indigenous peoples in California">History of enslavement of indigenous peoples in California</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavery_in_New_France" title="Slavery in New France">Slavery in New France</a></div>
<p>In the early years of the <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_Bay" title="Chesapeake Bay">Chesapeake Bay</a> settlements, colonial officials found it difficult to attract and retain laborers under the harsh frontier conditions, and there was a high mortality rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hofstadter_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hofstadter-7">[7]</a></sup> Most laborers came from Britain as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indentured_servants" title="Indentured servants">indentured servants</a>, having signed contracts of indenture to pay with work for their passage, their upkeep and training, usually on a farm. The colonies had agricultural economies. These indentured servants were young people who intended to become permanent residents. In some cases, convicted criminals were transported to the colonies as indentured servants, rather than being imprisoned. The indentured servants were not slaves, but were required to work for four to seven years in Virginia to pay the cost of their passage and maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Many Germans, Scots-Irish, and Irish came to the colonies in the 18th century, settling in the backcountry of Pennsylvania and further south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hofstadter_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hofstadter-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right;">

<p>The first 19 or so Africans to reach the English colonies arrived in <a href="/wiki/Jamestown,_Virginia" title="Jamestown, Virginia">Jamestown, Virginia</a> in 1619, brought by Dutch traders who had seized them from a captured Spanish <a href="/wiki/Slave_ship" title="Slave ship">slave ship</a>. The Spanish usually baptized slaves in Africa before embarking them. As English custom then considered baptized Christians exempt from slavery, colonists treated these Africans as indentured servants, and they joined about 1,000 English indentured servants already in the colony. The Africans were freed after a prescribed period and given the use of land and supplies by their former masters. The historian <a href="/wiki/Ira_Berlin" title="Ira Berlin">Ira Berlin</a> noted that what he called the "charter generation" in the colonies was sometimes made up of mixed-race men (Atlantic Creoles) who were indentured servants, and whose ancestry was African and Iberian. They were descendants of African women and Portuguese or Spanish men who worked in African ports as traders or facilitators in the slave trade. For example, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Johnson_(colonist)" title="Anthony Johnson (colonist)">Anthony Johnson</a> arrived in Virginia in 1621 from Angola as an indentured servant; he became free and a property owner, eventually buying and owning slaves himself. The transformation of the social status of Africans, from indentured servitude to slaves in a racial caste which they could not leave or escape, happened gradually.</p>
<p>There were no laws regarding slavery early in Virginia's history. But, in 1640, a Virginia court sentenced <a href="/wiki/John_Punch_(slave)" title="John Punch (slave)">John Punch</a>, an African, to slavery after he attempted to flee his service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donoghue_2010_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donoghue_2010-10">[10]</a></sup> The two whites with whom he fled were sentenced only to an additional year of their indenture, and three years' service to the colony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> This marked the first legal sanctioning of slavery in the English colonies and was one of the first legal distinctions made between Europeans and Africans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donoghue_2010_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Donoghue_2010-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1670_virginia_tobacco_slaves.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="793" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/1670_virginia_tobacco_slaves.jpg/220px-1670_virginia_tobacco_slaves.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/1670_virginia_tobacco_slaves.jpg/330px-1670_virginia_tobacco_slaves.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/1670_virginia_tobacco_slaves.jpg/440px-1670_virginia_tobacco_slaves.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

<p>In 1641, Massachusetts became the first colony to authorize slavery through enacted law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Higginbotham1975_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Higginbotham1975-14">[14]</a></sup> Massachusetts passed the Body of Liberties, which prohibited slavery in many instances, but did allow for three legal bases of slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Higginbotham1975_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Higginbotham1975-14">[14]</a></sup> Slaves could be held if they were captives of war, if they sold themselves into slavery or were purchased from elsewhere, or if they were sentenced to slavery as punishment by the governing authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Higginbotham1975_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Higginbotham1975-14">[14]</a></sup> The Body of Liberties used the word "strangers" to refer to people bought and sold as slaves; they were generally not English subjects. Colonists came to equate this term with Native Americans and Africans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiecek1977_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wiecek1977-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1654, <a href="/wiki/John_Casor" title="John Casor">John Casor</a>, a black indentured servant in colonial Virginia, was the first man to be declared a slave in a civil case. He had claimed to an officer that his master, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Johnson_(colonist)" title="Anthony Johnson (colonist)">Anthony Johnson</a>, himself a <a href="/wiki/Free_people_of_color" title="Free people of color">free black</a>, had held him past his indenture term. A neighbor, Robert Parker told Johnson that if he did not release Casor, Parker would testify in court to this fact. Under local laws, Johnson was at risk for losing some of his <a href="/wiki/Headright" title="Headright">headright</a> lands for violating the terms of indenture. Under duress, Johnson freed Casor. Casor entered into a seven years' indenture with Parker. Feeling cheated, Johnson sued Parker to repossess Casor. A <a href="/wiki/Northampton_County,_Virginia" title="Northampton County, Virginia">Northampton County, Virginia</a> court ruled for Johnson, declaring that Parker illegally was detaining Casor from his rightful master who legally held him "for the duration of his life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the colonial period, the status of slaves was affected by interpretations related to the status of foreigners in England. England had no system of naturalizing immigrants to its island or its colonies. Since persons of African origins were not English subjects by birth, they were among those peoples considered foreigners and generally outside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_common_law" title="English common law">English common law</a>. The colonies struggled with how to classify people born to foreigners and subjects. In 1656 Virginia, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Key_Grinstead" title="Elizabeth Key Grinstead">Elizabeth Key Grinstead</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed-race" title="Mixed-race">mixed-race</a> woman, successfully gained her freedom and that of her son in a challenge to her status by making her case as the baptized Christian daughter of the free Englishman Thomas Key. Her attorney was an English subject, which may have helped her case. (He was also the father of her mixed-race son, and the couple married after Key was freed.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banks_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Banks-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slave_dance_to_banjo,_1780s.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="651" data-file-width="982" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Slave_dance_to_banjo%2C_1780s.jpg/220px-Slave_dance_to_banjo%2C_1780s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Slave_dance_to_banjo%2C_1780s.jpg/330px-Slave_dance_to_banjo%2C_1780s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Slave_dance_to_banjo%2C_1780s.jpg/440px-Slave_dance_to_banjo%2C_1780s.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slave_dance_to_banjo,_1780s.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Slaves on a South Carolina plantation (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Old_Plantation" title="The Old Plantation">The Old Plantation</a></i>, c. 1790)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shortly after the Elizabeth Key trial and similar challenges, in 1662 the Virginia <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_colony" title="Royal colony">royal colony</a> approved a law adopting the principle of <i><a href="/wiki/Partus_sequitur_ventrem" title="Partus sequitur ventrem">partus sequitur ventrem</a></i> (called <i>partus</i>, for short), stating that any children born in the colony would take the status of the mother. A child of an enslaved mother would be born into slavery, regardless if the father were a freeborn Englishman or Christian. This was a reversal of <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> practice in England, which ruled that children of English subjects took the status of the father. The change institutionalized the skewed power relationships between slaveowners and slave women, freed the white men from the legal responsibility to acknowledge or financially support their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed-race" title="Mixed-race">mixed-race</a> children, and somewhat confined the open scandal of mixed-race children and <a href="/wiki/Miscegenation" title="Miscegenation">miscegenation</a> to within the slave quarters.</p>
<p>The Virginia <a href="/wiki/Slave_codes" title="Slave codes">Slave codes</a> of 1705 further defined as slaves those people imported from nations that were not <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a>. <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> who were sold to colonists by other Native Americans (from rival tribes), or captured by Europeans during village raids, were also defined as slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amslav_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-amslav-18">[18]</a></sup> This codified the earlier principle of non-Christian foreigner enslavement.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charges_and_net_proceed_of_118_new_Negroe_slaves_Charleston_South_Carolina.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="1085" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Charges_and_net_proceed_of_118_new_Negroe_slaves_Charleston_South_Carolina.jpg/150px-Charges_and_net_proceed_of_118_new_Negroe_slaves_Charleston_South_Carolina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Charges_and_net_proceed_of_118_new_Negroe_slaves_Charleston_South_Carolina.jpg/225px-Charges_and_net_proceed_of_118_new_Negroe_slaves_Charleston_South_Carolina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Charges_and_net_proceed_of_118_new_Negroe_slaves_Charleston_South_Carolina.jpg/300px-Charges_and_net_proceed_of_118_new_Negroe_slaves_Charleston_South_Carolina.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charges_and_net_proceed_of_118_new_Negroe_slaves_Charleston_South_Carolina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ledger" title="Ledger">Ledger</a> of sale of 118 slaves, <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston, South Carolina</a>, c. 1754</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1735, the <a href="/wiki/Trustees_for_the_Establishment_of_the_Colony_of_Georgia_in_America" title="Trustees for the Establishment of the Colony of Georgia in America">Georgia Trustees</a> enacted a law to prohibit slavery in the new colony, which had been established in 1733 to enable the "worthy poor" as well as persecuted European Protestants to have a new start. Slavery was then legal in the other twelve English colonies. Neighboring South Carolina had an economy based on the use of enslaved labor. The Georgia Trustees wanted to eliminate the risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_rebellions" title="Slave rebellions">slave rebellions</a> and make Georgia better able to defend against attacks from the Spanish to the south, who offered freedom to escaped slaves. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Edward_Oglethorpe" title="James Edward Oglethorpe">James Edward Oglethorpe</a> was the driving force behind the colony, and the only trustee to reside in Georgia. He opposed slavery on moral grounds as well as for pragmatic reasons, and vigorously defended the ban on slavery against fierce opposition from Carolina slave merchants and land speculators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cornerstones_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-cornerstones-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a> highlanders who settled what is now <a href="/wiki/Darien,_Georgia" title="Darien, Georgia">Darien, Georgia</a> added a moral anti-slavery argument, which became increasingly rare in the South, in their 1739 "Petition of the Inhabitants of New Inverness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> By 1750 Georgia authorized slavery in the state because they had been unable to secure enough indentured servants as laborers. As economic conditions in England began to improve in the first half of the 18th century, workers had no reason to leave, especially to face the risks in the colonies.</p>
<p>During most of the British colonial period, slavery existed in all the colonies. People enslaved in <a href="/wiki/Northern_United_States" title="Northern United States">the North</a> typically worked as house servants, artisans, laborers and craftsmen, with the greater number in cities. Many men worked on the docks and in shipping. In 1703, more than 42 percent of New York City households held slaves, the second-highest proportion of any city in the colonies after <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston, South Carolina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nation_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nation-23">[23]</a></sup> But slaves were also used as agricultural workers in farm communities, including in areas of upstate New York and Long Island, Connecticut and New Jersey.</p>
<p>The South developed an agricultural economy dependent on commodity crops. Its planters rapidly acquired a significantly higher number and proportion of slaves in the population overall, as its commodity crops were labor-intensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usnews.com_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-usnews.com-24">[24]</a></sup> Early on, enslaved people in <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">the South</a> worked primarily in agriculture, on farms and plantations growing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigo_plant" title="Indigo plant">indigo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a>; <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a> did not become a major crop until after the American Revolution and after the 1790s. Before then long-staple cotton was cultivated primarily on the Sea Islands of Georgia and South Carolina.</p>
<p>The invention of the <a href="/wiki/Cotton_gin" title="Cotton gin">cotton gin</a> in 1793 enabled the cultivation of short-staple cotton in a wide variety of mainland areas, leading in the 19th century to the development of large areas of the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a> as cotton country. Tobacco was very labor-intensive, as was rice cultivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-25">[25]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Province_of_South_Carolina" title="Province of South Carolina">South Carolina</a> in 1720, about 65% of the population consisted of enslaved people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Planters (defined by historians in the Upper South as those who held 20 enslaved people or more) used enslaved workers to cultivate commodity crops. They also worked in the artisanal trades on large plantations and in many southern port cities. Backwoods subsistence farmers, the later wave of settlers in the 18th century who settled along the Appalachian Mountains and backcountry, seldom held enslaved people.</p>
<p>Some of the British colonies attempted to abolish the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">international slave trade</a>, fearing that the importation of new Africans would be disruptive. Virginia bills to that effect were vetoed by the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">British Privy Council</a>. <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Rhode_Island_and_Providence_Plantations" title="Colony of Rhode Island and Providence Plantations">Rhode Island</a> forbade the import of enslaved people in 1774. All of the colonies except <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Georgia" title="Province of Georgia">Georgia</a> had banned or limited the African slave trade by 1786; Georgia did so in 1798. Some of these laws were later repealed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fewer than 350,000 enslaved people were imported into the Thirteen Colonies and the U.S, constituting less than 5% of the twelve million enslaved people brought from Africa to the Americas. The great majority of enslaved Africans were transported to sugar colonies in the Caribbean and to Brazil. As life expectancy was short, their numbers had to be continually replenished. Life expectancy was much higher in the U.S., and the enslaved population was successful in reproduction. The number of enslaved people in the US grew rapidly, reaching <span class="nowrap">4 million</span> by the 1860 Census. From 1770 until 1860, the rate of natural growth of North American enslaved people was much greater than for the population of any nation in Europe, and it was nearly twice as rapid as that of England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Louisiana">Louisiana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Louisiana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Louisiana was founded as a French colony. Colonial officials in 1724 implemented <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV of France</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Code_Noir" title="Code Noir">Code Noir</a></i>, which regulated the slave trade and the institution of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavery_in_New_France" title="Slavery in New France">slavery in New France</a> and French Caribbean colonies. This resulted in a different pattern of slavery in Louisiana, purchased in 1803, compared to the rest of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.fr_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.fr-29">[29]</a></sup> As written, the <i>Code Noir</i> gave some rights to slaves, including the right to marry. Although it authorized and codified cruel corporal punishment against slaves under certain conditions, it forbade slave owners to torture them or to separate married couples (or to separate young children from their mothers). It also required the owners to instruct slaves in the Catholic faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodney_Stark_p.322_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rodney_Stark_p.322-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FL6oMC_p.115_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FL6oMC_p.115-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Together with a more permeable historic French system that allowed certain rights to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gens_de_couleur_libres" title="Gens de couleur libres">gens de couleur libres</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Free_people_of_color" title="Free people of color">free people of color</a>), often born to white fathers and their mixed-race concubines, a far higher percentage of <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a> in Louisiana were free as of the 1830 census (13.2% in <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery_in_Louisiana" title="History of slavery in Louisiana">Louisiana</a> compared to 0.8% in <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, whose population was dominated by <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">white</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_American" title="English American">Anglo-Americans</a>. Most of Louisiana's "third class" of free people of color, situated between the native-born French and mass of African slaves, lived in New Orleans).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodney_Stark_p.322_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rodney_Stark_p.322-30">[30]</a></sup> The Louisiana free people of color were often literate, had gained education, and a significant number owned businesses, properties, and even slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FL6oMC_p.115_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FL6oMC_p.115-32">[32]</a></sup> The <i>Code Noir</i> forbade interracial marriages. However, <a href="/wiki/Pla%C3%A7age" title="Plaçage">interracial unions were widespread</a> under the system known as placage. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed-race" title="Mixed-race">mixed-race</a> offspring (cf. <a href="/wiki/Creoles_of_color" title="Creoles of color">creoles of color</a>) from such unions were among those in the intermediate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_caste" title="Social caste">social caste</a> of free people of color. The English colonies insisted on a binary system, in which <a href="/wiki/Mulatto" title="Mulatto">mulatto</a> and black slaves were treated equally under the law, and discriminated against equally if free. But many free people of African descent were mixed race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.fr_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.fr-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FL6oMC_p.115_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-FL6oMC_p.115-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the US took over Louisiana, Americans from the Protestant South entered the territory and began to impose their norms. They officially discouraged interracial relationships (although white men continued to have unions with black women, both enslaved and free.) The <i>Americanization</i> of Louisiana gradually resulted in a binary system of race, causing free people of color to lose status as they were grouped with the slaves. They lost certain rights as they became classified by American whites as officially "black".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.fr_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.fr-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolutionary_era">Revolutionary era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Revolutionary era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable sortable" style="float:right;">

<td align="center">1.8</td>
</tr>

<p>Slavery in Great Britain had never been authorized by statute. In 1772 it was made partially unenforceable at <a href="/wiki/Slavery_at_common_law" title="Slavery at common law">common law</a> in Great Britain by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somersett%27s_Case" title="Somersett's Case">legal decision</a>. The large British role in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">international slave trade</a> continued until 1807. Slavery flourished in most of Britain's colonies, with many wealthy slave owners living in England and holding considerable power. They were bought out in 1833 and the slaves were emancipated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early 1775 <a href="/wiki/John_Murray,_4th_Earl_of_Dunmore" title="John Murray, 4th Earl of Dunmore">Lord Dunmore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colonial_government_in_the_Thirteen_Colonies" title="Colonial government in the Thirteen Colonies">royal governor</a> of Virginia, wrote to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Dartmouth" title="Lord Dartmouth">Lord Dartmouth</a> of his intent to free slaves owned by Patriots in case of rebellion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> On November 7, 1775, Lord Dunmore issued <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Dunmore%27s_Proclamation" title="Lord Dunmore's Proclamation">Lord Dunmore's Proclamation</a> which declared <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and promised freedom for any slaves of <a href="/wiki/Patriot_(American_Revolution)" title="Patriot (American Revolution)">American patriots</a> who would leave their masters and join the <a href="/wiki/British_Armed_Forces" title="British Armed Forces">royal forces</a>. Slaves owned by Loyalist masters, however, were unaffected by Dunmore's Proclamation. About 1500 slaves owned by Patriots escaped and joined Dunmore's forces. Most died of disease before they could do any fighting. Three hundred of these freed slaves made it to freedom in Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many slaves used the very disruption of war to escape their plantations and fade into cities or woods. For instance, in South Carolina, nearly 25,000 slaves (30% of the total enslaved population) fled, migrated or died during the war. Throughout the South, losses of slaves were high, with many due to escapes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Slaves also escaped throughout New England and the mid-Atlantic, joining the British who had occupied New York.</p>
<p>In the closing months of the war, the British evacuated 20,000 freedmen from major coastal cities, transporting more than 3,000 for resettlement in <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, where they were registered as Black Loyalists and eventually granted land. They transported others to the Caribbean islands, and some to England.</p>
<p>At the same time, the British were transporting Loyalists and their slaves, primarily to the Caribbean, but some to Nova Scotia. For example, over 5,000 enslaved Africans owned by Loyalists were transported in 1782 with their owners from Savannah to <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Augustine,_Florida" title="St. Augustine, Florida">St. Augustine, Florida</a> (then controlled by Britain). Similarly, over half of the black people evacuated in 1782 from Charleston by the British to the West Indies and Florida were slaves owned by white Loyalists.</p>
<p>Slaves and free blacks also fought on the side of rebels during the Revolutionary War. Washington authorized slaves to be freed who fought with the American Continental Army. Rhode Island started enlisting slaves in 1778, and promised compensation to owners whose slaves enlisted and survived to gain freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> During the course of the war, about one fifth of the northern army was black.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In 1781, Baron Closen, a <a href="/wiki/Germans_in_the_American_Revolution" title="Germans in the American Revolution">German officer</a> in the French <a href="/wiki/Royal_Deux-Ponts_Regiment" title="Royal Deux-Ponts Regiment">Royal Deux-Ponts Regiment</a> at the Battle of Yorktown, estimated the American army to be about one-quarter black.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> These men included both former slaves and free blacks.</p>
<p>In the 18th century, Britain became the world's largest slave trader. Starting in 1777, the Patriots outlawed the importation of slaves state by state. They all acted to end the international trade but it was later reopened in South Carolina and Georgia. In 1807 Congress acted on President Jefferson's advice and made importing slaves from abroad a federal crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitution_of_the_United_States">Constitution of the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Constitution of the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" title="Constitution of the United States">Constitution of the United States</a> took effect in 1789 and included several provisions regarding slavery. Section 9 of Article I forbade the Federal government from banning the importation of slaves before January 1, 1808. As a protection for slavery, the delegates approved Section 2 of Article IV, which prohibited states from freeing slaves who fled to them from another state, and required the return of chattel property to owners.</p>
<p>In a section negotiated by <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> of Virginia, Section 2 of Article I designated "other persons" (slaves) to be added to the total of the state's free population, at the rate of three-fifths of their total number, to establish the state's official population for the purposes of apportionment of Congressional representation and federal taxation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The protections afforded slavery in the Constitution disproportionately strengthened the political power of Southern representatives, as three-fifths of the (non-voting) slave population was counted for Congressional apportionment.</p>
<p>In addition, many parts of the country were tied to the Southern economy. As the historian James Oliver Horton noted, prominent slaveholder politicians and the commodity crops of the South had a strong influence on United States politics and economy. Horton said,</p>
<blockquote>
<p>in the 72 years between the election of George Washington and the election of Abraham Lincoln, 50 of those years [had] a slaveholder as president of the United States, and, for that whole period of time, there was never a person elected to a second term who was not a slaveholder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>This increased the power of southern states in Congress for decades, affecting national policies and legislation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-47">[47]</a></sup> The planter elite dominated the southern Congressional delegations and the United States presidency for nearly 50 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs.org_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1790_to_1850">1790 to 1850</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1790 to 1850">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_and_free_states" title="Slave and free states">Slave and free states</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abolition_in_the_North">Abolition in the North</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Abolition in the North">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">Abolitionism in the United States</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abolition_of_slavery_timeline" title="Abolition of slavery timeline">Abolition of slavery timeline</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_notable_opponents_of_slavery" title="List of notable opponents of slavery">List of notable opponents of slavery</a></div>
<p>Beginning during the revolution and in the first two decades of the postwar era, every state in the North abolished slavery, ending with New Jersey in 1804, although in some cases existing slaves were not liberated immediately. These were the first abolitionist laws in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_World" title="Atlantic World">Atlantic World</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Massachusetts, slavery was successfully challenged in court in 1783 in a <a href="/wiki/Freedom_suit" title="Freedom suit">freedom suit</a> by <a href="/wiki/Quock_Walker" title="Quock Walker">Quock Walker</a>; he said that slavery was in contradiction to the state's new constitution of 1780 providing for equality of men. Freed slaves were subject to <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation_in_the_United_States#Issues_in_the_North" title="Racial segregation in the United States">racial segregation</a> and discrimination in the North, and it took decades for some states to extend the franchise to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIA4_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-AIA4-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most northern states passed legislation for gradual abolition, first freeing children born to slave mothers (and requiring them to serve lengthy indentures to their mother's masters, often into their 20s as young adults). As a result of this gradualist approach, New York did not fully free its last ex-slaves until 1827, Rhode Island had seven slaves still listed in the 1840 census. Pennsylvania's last ex-slaves were freed in 1847, Connecticut's in 1848, and New Hampshire and New Jersey in 1865.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>None of the Southern states abolished slavery, but it was common for individual slaveholders in the South to free numerous slaves, often citing revolutionary ideals, in their wills. Methodist, Quaker and Baptist preachers traveled in the South, appealing to slaveholders to <a href="/wiki/Manumission" title="Manumission">manumit</a> their slaves. By 1810, the number and proportion of free blacks in the population of the United States had risen dramatically. Most free blacks resided in the North, but even in the Upper South, the proportion of free blacks went from less than one percent of all blacks to more than 10 percent, even as the total number of slaves was increasing through importation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_Kolchin_1993_pp._77-78_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_Kolchin_1993_pp._77-78-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Through the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">Northwest Ordinance</a> of 1787 under the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a>, slavery was prohibited in the territories northwest of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a>; existing slaves were not freed for years, although they could no longer be sold. This was a compromise. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> proposed in 1784 to end slavery in all the territories, but his bill lost in the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress</a> by one vote. The territories south of the Ohio River (and Missouri) had authorized slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Northerners predominated in the westward movement into the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwestern</a> territory after the American Revolution; as the states were organized, they voted to prohibit slavery in their constitutions when they achieved statehood: Ohio in 1803, Indiana in 1816, and Illinois in 1818. What developed was a Northern block of free states united into one contiguous geographic area that generally shared an anti-slavery culture. The exceptions were the areas along the Ohio River settled by Southerners, the southern portions of states such as Indiana, Ohio and Illinois. Residents of those areas generally shared in Southern culture and attitudes. In addition, these areas were devoted to agriculture longer than the industrializing northern parts of these states, and some farmers used slave labor. The emancipation of slaves in the North led to the growth in the population of northern free blacks, from several hundred in the 1770s to nearly 50,000 by 1810.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Agitation_against_slavery">Agitation against slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Agitation against slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Legree.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="760" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Legree.png/220px-Legree.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Legree.png/330px-Legree.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Legree.png/440px-Legree.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Legree.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Simon Legree and Uncle Tom: A scene from <i><a href="/wiki/Uncle_Tom%27s_Cabin" title="Uncle Tom's Cabin">Uncle Tom's Cabin</a></i> (1852), history's most famous abolitionist novel</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Throughout the first half of the 19th century, abolitionism, a movement to end slavery, grew in strength; most abolitionist societies and supporters were in the North. They worked to raise awareness about the evils of slavery, and to build support for abolition.</p>
<p>This struggle took place amid strong support for slavery among white Southerners, who profited greatly from the system of enslaved labor. But slavery was entwined with the national economy; for instance, the banking, shipping and manufacturing industries of New York City all had strong economic interests in slavery, as did similar industries in other major port cities in the North. The northern textile mills in New York and New England processed Southern cotton and manufactured clothes to outfit slaves. By 1822 half of New York City's exports were related to cotton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-divided_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-divided-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slaveholders began to refer to slavery as the "<a href="/wiki/Peculiar_institution" title="Peculiar institution">peculiar institution</a>" to differentiate it from other examples of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forced_labor" title="Forced labor">forced labor</a>. They justified it as less cruel than the free labor of the North.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HClay.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="763" data-file-width="610" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/HClay.jpg/150px-HClay.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/HClay.jpg/225px-HClay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/HClay.jpg/300px-HClay.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HClay.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> (1777–1852), one of three founders of the <a href="/wiki/American_Colonization_Society" title="American Colonization Society">American Colonization Society</a>, intended to relocate free blacks from the US to Africa, founding <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-AFP-56">[56]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The principal organized bodies to advocate abolition and anti-slavery reforms in the north were the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Abolition_Society" title="Pennsylvania Abolition Society">Pennsylvania Abolition Society</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Manumission_Society" title="New York Manumission Society">New York Manumission Society</a>. Before the 1830s the antislavery groups called for gradual emancipation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> By the late 1820s, under the impulse of religious evangelicals, the sense emerged that owning slaves was a sin and the owner had to immediately free himself from this grave sin by emancipation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonization_movement">Colonization movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Colonization movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the early part of the 19th century, other organizations were founded to take action on the future of black Americans. Some advocated removing free black people from the United States to places where they would enjoy greater freedom; some endorsed <a href="/wiki/Colony" title="Colony">colonization</a> in Africa, while others advocated <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">emigration</a>. During the 1820s and 1830s, the <a href="/wiki/American_Colonization_Society" title="American Colonization Society">American Colonization Society</a> (ACS) was the primary organization to implement the "return" of black Americans to Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-AFP-56">[56]</a></sup> The ACS was made up mostly of <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a> and slaveholders, who found uneasy common ground in support of "repatriation". But, by this time, most black Americans were native-born and did not want to emigrate; rather, they wanted full rights in the United States, where their people had lived and worked for generations.</p>
<p>In 1822 the ACS established the colony of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> in West Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WDLMap_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-WDLMap-59">[59]</a></sup> The ACS assisted thousands of freedmen and free blacks (with legislated limits) to emigrate there from the United States. Many white people considered this preferable to <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">emancipation</a> in the United States. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, one of the founders and a prominent slaveholder politician from Kentucky, said that blacks faced</p>
<blockquote>
<p>unconquerable prejudice resulting from their color, they never could amalgamate with the free whites of this country. It was desirable, therefore, as it respected them, and the residue of the population of the country, to drain them off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATH_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-ATH-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>After 1830, abolitionist and minister <a href="/wiki/William_Lloyd_Garrison" title="William Lloyd Garrison">William Lloyd Garrison</a> promoted emancipation, characterizing slaveholding as a personal sin. He demanded that slaveowners repent and start the process of emancipation. His position increased defensiveness on the part of some southerners, who noted the long history of slavery among many cultures. A few abolitionists, such as <a href="/wiki/John_Brown_(abolitionist)" title="John Brown (abolitionist)">John Brown</a>, favored the use of armed force to foment uprisings among the slaves, as he did at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Ferry" title="Harper's Ferry">Harper's Ferry</a>. Most abolitionists tried to raise public support to change laws and to challenge slave laws. Abolitionists were active on the lecture circuit in the North, and often featured escaped slaves in their presentations. The eloquent <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass">Frederick Douglass</a> became an important abolitionist leader after escaping from slavery. <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Beecher_Stowe" title="Harriet Beecher Stowe">Harriet Beecher Stowe</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Uncle_Tom%27s_Cabin" title="Uncle Tom's Cabin">Uncle Tom's Cabin</a></i> (1852) was an international bestseller and aroused popular sentiment against slavery. It also provoked the publication of numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Tom_novels" title="Anti-Tom novels">anti-Tom novels</a> by Southerners in the years before the American Civil War.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prohibiting_international_trade">Prohibiting international trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Prohibiting international trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While under the Constitution, Congress could not prohibit the import slave trade until 1808, the third Congress regulated it in the <a href="/wiki/Slave_Trade_Act_of_1794" title="Slave Trade Act of 1794">Slave Trade Act of 1794</a>, which prohibited shipbuilding and outfitting for the trade. Subsequent acts <a href="/wiki/Slave_Trade_Act_of_1800" title="Slave Trade Act of 1800">in 1800</a> and 1803 sought to discourage the trade by limiting investment in import trading and prohibiting importation into states that had abolished slavery, which most in the North had by that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Regulation_of_the_Trade_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Regulation_of_the_Trade-61">[61]</a></sup> The final <a href="/wiki/Act_Prohibiting_Importation_of_Slaves" title="Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves">Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves</a> was adopted in 1807, effective in 1808. However, illegal importation of African slaves (smuggling) was common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Julia_Floyd_Smith_1973.2C_pp._44-46_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Julia_Floyd_Smith_1973.2C_pp._44-46-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Great Britain and the United States outlawed the international slave trade in 1807, British slave trade suppression activities began in 1808 through diplomatic efforts and formation of the Royal Navy's <a href="/wiki/West_Africa_Squadron" title="West Africa Squadron">West Africa Squadron</a>. From 1819, they were assisted by forces from the United States Navy. With the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Webster-Ashburton_Treaty" title="Webster-Ashburton Treaty">Webster-Ashburton Treaty</a> of 1842, the relationship with Britain was formalized, and the two countries jointly ran the <a href="/wiki/Blockade_of_Africa" title="Blockade of Africa">Blockade of Africa</a> with their navies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-revolution_Southern_manumissions">Post-revolution Southern manumissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Post-revolution Southern manumissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although Virginia, Maryland and Delaware were slave states, the latter two already had a high proportion of free blacks by the outbreak of war. Following the Revolution, the three legislatures made <a href="/wiki/Manumission" title="Manumission">manumission</a> easier, allowed by deed or will. Quaker and Methodist ministers particularly urged slaveholders to free their slaves. The number and proportion of freed slaves in these states rose dramatically until 1810. More than half of the number of free blacks in the United States were concentrated in the Upper South. The proportion of free blacks among the black population in the Upper South rose from less than one percent in 1792 to more than 10 percent by 1810.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_Kolchin_1993_pp._77-78_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_Kolchin_1993_pp._77-78-52">[52]</a></sup> In Delaware, nearly 75 percent of blacks were free by 1810.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the US as a whole, by 1810 the number of free blacks reached 186,446, or 13.5 percent of all blacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PeterKolchin_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-PeterKolchin-64">[64]</a></sup> After that period, few slaves were freed, as the development of cotton plantations featuring short-staple cotton in the Deep South drove up the internal demand for slaves in the domestic slave trade and high prices were paid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_slave_trade_and_forced_migration">Domestic slave trade and forced migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Domestic slave trade and forced migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slavery_map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3551" data-file-width="2251" height="347" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f1/Slavery_map.jpg/220px-Slavery_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f1/Slavery_map.jpg/330px-Slavery_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f1/Slavery_map.jpg/440px-Slavery_map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slavery_map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Movement of slaves between 1790 and 1860</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The growing international demand for cotton led many plantation owners further west in search of suitable land. In addition, the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Cotton_gin" title="Cotton gin">cotton gin</a> in 1793 enabled profitable processing of short-staple cotton, which could readily be grown in the uplands. The invention revolutionized the cotton industry by increasing fifty-fold the quantity of cotton that could be processed in a day. At the end of the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, fewer than 300,000 bales of cotton were produced nationally. By 1820 the amount of cotton produced had increased to 600,000 bales, and by 1850 it had reached 4,000,000. There was an explosive growth of cotton cultivation throughout the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a> and greatly increased demand for slave labor to support it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> As a result, manumissions decreased dramatically in the South.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crowe-Slaves_Waiting_for_Sale_-_Richmond,_Virginia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="406" data-file-width="600" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Crowe-Slaves_Waiting_for_Sale_-_Richmond%2C_Virginia.jpg/250px-Crowe-Slaves_Waiting_for_Sale_-_Richmond%2C_Virginia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Crowe-Slaves_Waiting_for_Sale_-_Richmond%2C_Virginia.jpg/375px-Crowe-Slaves_Waiting_for_Sale_-_Richmond%2C_Virginia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Crowe-Slaves_Waiting_for_Sale_-_Richmond%2C_Virginia.jpg/500px-Crowe-Slaves_Waiting_for_Sale_-_Richmond%2C_Virginia.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crowe-Slaves_Waiting_for_Sale_-_Richmond,_Virginia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Slaves Waiting for Sale: Richmond, Virginia</i>. Painted upon the sketch of 1853</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most of the slaves sold from the Upper South were from <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolinas" title="Carolinas">Carolinas</a>, where changes in agriculture decreased the need for their labor and the demand for slaves. Before 1810, primary destinations for the slaves who were sold were <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, but after 1810 <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> of the Deep South received the most slaves. This is where cotton became king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Kentucky and Tennessee joined the slave exporting states.</p>
<p>By 1815, the domestic slave trade had become a major economic activity in the United States; it lasted until the 1860s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CUP_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-CUP-69">[69]</a></sup> Between 1830 and 1840 nearly 250,000 slaves were taken across state lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CUP_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-CUP-69">[69]</a></sup> In the 1850s more than 193,000 were transported, and historians estimate nearly one million in total took part in the forced migration of this new Middle Passage. By 1860 the slave population in the United States had reached 4 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CUP_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-CUP-69">[69]</a></sup> Of all 1,515,605 free families in the fifteen slave states in 1860, nearly 400,000 held slaves (roughly one in four, or 25%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> amounting to 8% of all American families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ashley%27s_Sack_(Slave_Sack_c._mid-19th_century).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2496" data-file-width="1944" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ashley%27s_Sack_%28Slave_Sack_c._mid-19th_century%29.jpg/220px-Ashley%27s_Sack_%28Slave_Sack_c._mid-19th_century%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ashley%27s_Sack_%28Slave_Sack_c._mid-19th_century%29.jpg/330px-Ashley%27s_Sack_%28Slave_Sack_c._mid-19th_century%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Ashley%27s_Sack_%28Slave_Sack_c._mid-19th_century%29.jpg/440px-Ashley%27s_Sack_%28Slave_Sack_c._mid-19th_century%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ashley%27s_Sack_(Slave_Sack_c._mid-19th_century).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ashley%27s_Sack" title="Ashley's Sack">Ashley's Sack</a> is a cloth that recounts a slave sale separating a mother and her daughter. The sack belonged to a nine-year-old girl Ashley which was a parting gift from her mother, Rose, after Ashley had been sold. Rose filled the sack with a dress, braid of her hair, pecans, and "my love always"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Ira_Berlin" title="Ira Berlin">Ira Berlin</a> called this forced migration of slaves the "Second Middle Passage", because it reproduced many of the same horrors as the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Passage" title="Middle Passage">Middle Passage</a> (the name given to the transportation of slaves from Africa to North America). These sales of slaves broke up many families and caused much hardship. Characterizing it as the "central event" in the life of a slave between the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> and the Civil War, Berlin wrote that whether slaves were directly uprooted or lived in fear that they or their families would be involuntarily moved, "the massive deportation traumatized black people, both slave and free."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Individuals lost their connection to families and clans. Added to the earlier colonists combining slaves from different tribes, many ethnic Africans lost their knowledge of varying tribal origins in Africa. Most were descended from families who had been in the United States for many generations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CUP_69-3"><a href="#cite_note-CUP-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1840s, almost 300,000 slaves were transported, with Alabama and Mississippi receiving 100,000 each. During each decade between 1810 and 1860, at least 100,000 slaves were moved from their state of origin. In the final decade before the Civil War, 250,000 were moved. Michael Tadman wrote in <i>Speculators and Slaves: Masters, Traders, and Slaves in the Old South</i> (1989) that 60–70% of inter-regional migrations were the result of the sale of slaves. In 1820 a child in the Upper South had a 30% chance of being sold south by 1860.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The death rate for the slaves on their way to their new destination across the American South was less than that suffered by captives shipped across the Atlantic Ocean, but mortality was higher than the normal death rate.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slave_Market-Atlanta_Georgia_1864.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="526" data-file-width="650" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Slave_Market-Atlanta_Georgia_1864.jpg/220px-Slave_Market-Atlanta_Georgia_1864.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Slave_Market-Atlanta_Georgia_1864.jpg/330px-Slave_Market-Atlanta_Georgia_1864.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Slave_Market-Atlanta_Georgia_1864.jpg/440px-Slave_Market-Atlanta_Georgia_1864.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slave_Market-Atlanta_Georgia_1864.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Slave trader's business in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, 1864.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Interregional_slave_trade" title="Interregional slave trade">Slave traders</a> transported two-thirds of the slaves who moved west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Only a minority moved with their families and existing master. Slave traders had little interest in purchasing or transporting intact slave families; in the early years, planters demanded only the young male slaves needed for heavy labor. Later, in the interest of creating a "self-reproducing labor force", planters purchased nearly equal numbers of men and women. Berlin wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The internal slave trade became the largest enterprise in the South outside the plantation itself, and probably the most advanced in its employment of modern transportation, finance, and publicity. The slave trade industry developed its own unique language, with terms such as "prime hands, bucks, breeding wenches, and "fancy girls" coming into common use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The expansion of the interstate slave trade contributed to the "economic revival of once depressed seaboard states" as demand accelerated the value of slaves who were subject to sale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some traders moved their "chattels" by sea, with <a href="/wiki/Norfolk,_Virginia" title="Norfolk, Virginia">Norfolk</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Orleans,_Louisiana" title="New Orleans, Louisiana">New Orleans</a> being the most common route, but most slaves were forced to walk overland. Others were shipped downriver from such markets as <a href="/wiki/Louisville,_Kentucky" title="Louisville, Kentucky">Louisville</a> on the Ohio River, and <a href="/wiki/Natchez,_Mississippi" title="Natchez, Mississippi">Natchez</a> on the Mississippi. Traders created regular migration routes served by a network of slave pens, yards, and warehouses needed as temporary housing for the slaves. In addition, other vendors provided clothes, food, and supplies for slaves. As the trek advanced, some slaves were sold and new ones purchased. Berlin concluded, "In all, the slave trade, with its hubs and regional centers, its spurs and circuits, reached into every cranny of southern society. Few southerners, black or white, were untouched."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Once the trip ended, slaves faced a life on the frontier significantly different from most labor in the Upper South. Clearing trees and starting crops on virgin fields was harsh and backbreaking work. A combination of inadequate nutrition, bad water, and exhaustion from both the journey and the work weakened the newly arrived slaves and produced casualties. New plantations were located at rivers' edges for ease of transportation and travel. <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">Mosquitoes</a> and other environmental challenges spread disease, which took the lives of many slaves. They had acquired only limited immunities to lowland diseases in their previous homes. The death rate was so high that, in the first few years of hewing a plantation out of the wilderness, some planters preferred whenever possible to use rented slaves rather than their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>The harsh conditions on the frontier increased slave resistance and led owners and overseers to rely on violence for control. Many of the slaves were new to cotton fields and unaccustomed to the "sunrise-to-sunset gang labor" required by their new life. Slaves were driven much harder than when they had been in growing tobacco or <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> back east. Slaves had less time and opportunity to improve the quality of their lives by raising their own <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> or tending vegetable gardens, for either their own consumption or trade, as they could in the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/French_Louisiana" title="French Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, French colonists had established <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a> plantations and exported sugar as the chief commodity crop. After the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a> in 1803, Americans entered the state and joined the sugar cultivation. Between 1810 and 1830, planters bought slaves from the North and the number of slaves increased from less than 10,000 to more than 42,000. Planters preferred young males, who represented two-thirds of the slave purchases. Dealing with sugar cane was even more physically demanding than growing cotton. The largely young, unmarried male slave force made the reliance on violence by the owners "especially savage".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> became nationally important as a slave market and port, as slaves were shipped from there upriver by <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steamboat</a> to plantations on the Mississippi River; it also sold slaves who had been shipped downriver from markets such as Louisville. By 1840, it had the largest slave market in North America. It became the wealthiest and the fourth-largest city in the nation, based chiefly on the slave trade and associated businesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The trading season was from September to May, after the harvest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slave traders were men of low reputation, even in the South. In the 1828 presidential election, candidate <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> was strongly criticized by opponents as a slave trader who transacted in slaves in defiance of modern standards or morality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Treatment_of_slaves_in_the_United_States" title="Treatment of slaves in the United States">Treatment of slaves in the United States</a></div>
<p>The treatment of slaves in the United States varied widely depending on conditions, times and places. The power relationships of slavery corrupted many whites who had authority over slaves, with children showing their own cruelty. Masters and overseers resorted to physical punishments to impose their wills. Slaves were punished by whipping, shackling, hanging, beating, burning, mutilation, branding and imprisonment. Punishment was most often meted out in response to disobedience or perceived infractions, but sometimes abuse was carried out to re-assert the dominance of the master or overseer of the slave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Treatment was usually harsher on large plantations, which were often managed by overseers and owned by absentee slaveholders, conditions permitting abuses.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/William_Wells_Brown" title="William Wells Brown">William Wells Brown</a>, who escaped to freedom, reported that on one plantation, slave men were required to pick 80 pounds per day of cotton, while women were required to pick 70 pounds; if any slave failed in his or her quota, they were subject to whip lashes for each pound they were short. The whipping post stood next to the cotton scales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> A New York man who attended a slave auction in the mid-19th century reported that at least three-quarters of the male slaves he saw at sale had scars on their backs from whipping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McInnis2011_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-McInnis2011-86">[86]</a></sup> By contrast, small slave-owning families had closer relationships between the owners and slaves; this sometimes resulted in a more humane environment but was not a given.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_M._Friedman" title="Lawrence M. Friedman">Lawrence M.
