</table>
<p>For various reasons, the census did not always include all of the slaves, especially in the West. California was admitted as a free state and reported no slaves. However, there were many slaves that were brought to work in the mines during the <a href="/wiki/California_Gold_Rush" title="California Gold Rush">California Gold Rush</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> Some Californian communities openly tolerated slavery, such as <a href="/wiki/History_of_San_Bernardino,_California#Mormon_San_Bernardino" title="History of San Bernardino, California">San Bernardino</a>, which was mostly made up of transplants from the neighboring slave <a href="/wiki/Utah_Territory" title="Utah Territory">territory of Utah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico_Territory" title="New Mexico Territory">New Mexico Territory</a> never reported any slaves on the census, yet sued the government for compensation for 600 slaves that were freed when congress outlawed slavery in the territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Snodgrass_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-Snodgrass-223">[223]</a></sup> Utah was actively trying to hide its slave population from Congress<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> and did not report slaves in several communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> Additionally, the census did not traditionally include Native Americans, and hence did not include Native American slaves or black slaves owned by Native Americans. There were hundreds of Native American slaves in California,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> Utah<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morrill_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-morrill-228">[228]</a></sup> and New Mexico<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Snodgrass_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-Snodgrass-223">[223]</a></sup> that were never recorded in the census.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_of_slaveholders">Distribution of slaveholders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Distribution of slaveholders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of the <a href="/wiki/1860_United_States_Census" title="1860 United States Census">1860 Census</a>, one may compute the following statistics on slaveholding:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Enumerating slave schedules by county, 393,975 named persons held 3,950,546 unnamed slaves, for an average of about ten slaves per holder. As some large holders held slaves in multiple counties and are thus multiply counted, this slightly overestimates the number of slaveholders.</li>
<li>Excluding slaves, the 1860 U.S. population was 27,167,529, yielding about 1 in 70 free persons (1.5%) being slaveholders. By counting only named slaveowners, this approach does not acknowledge people who benefited from slavery by being in a slaveowning household, e.g. the wife and children of an owner. Only 8% of all US families owned slaves,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> while in the South, 33% of families owned slaves. According to historian Joseph Glatthaar, the number of soldiers of the Confederacy's Army of Northern Virginia who either owned slaves or came from slave owning households is "almost one of every two 1861 recruits". In addition he notes that, "Untold numbers of enlistees rented land from, sold crops to, or worked for slaveholders. In the final tabulation, the vast majority of the volunteers of 1861 had a direct connection to slavery."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup></li>
<li>The distribution of slaves among holders was very unequal: holders of 200 or more slaves, constituting less than 1% of all US slaveholders (fewer than 4,000 persons, 1 in 7,000 free persons, or 0.015% of the population) held an estimated 20–30% of all slaves (800,000 to 1,200,000 slaves). Nineteen holders of 500 or more slaves have been identified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-largest_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-largest-232">[232]</a></sup> The largest slaveholder was <a href="/wiki/Joshua_John_Ward" title="Joshua John Ward">Joshua John Ward</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_South_Carolina" title="Georgetown, South Carolina">Georgetown, South Carolina</a>, who in 1850 held 1,092 slaves,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dap2008_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-dap2008-233">[233]</a></sup> and whose heirs in 1860 held 1,130 or 1,131 slaves<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-largest_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-largest-232">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dap2008_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-dap2008-233">[233]</a></sup> – he was dubbed "the king of the rice planters",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dap2008_233-2"><a href="#cite_note-dap2008-233">[233]</a></sup> and one of his plantations is now part of <a href="/wiki/Brookgreen_Gardens" title="Brookgreen Gardens">Brookgreen Gardens</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_the_United_States#Slavery_and_black_history" title="Historiography of the United States">Historiography of the United States § Slavery and black history</a></div>
<p>The historian <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kolchin" title="Peter Kolchin">Peter Kolchin</a>, writing in 1993, noted that until the latter decades of the 20th century, historians of slavery had primarily concerned themselves with the culture, practices and economics of the slaveholders, not with the slaves. This was in part due to the circumstance that most slaveholders were literate and left behind written records, whereas slaves were largely illiterate and not in a position to leave written records. Scholars differed as to whether slavery should be considered a benign or a "harshly exploitive" institution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kolchin_p._134_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kolchin_p._134-234">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p>Much of the history written prior to the 1950s had a distinctive racist slant to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kolchin_p._134_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kolchin_p._134-234">[234]</a></sup> By the 1970s and 1980s, historians were using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological" title="Archaeological">archaeological</a> records, black <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a>, and statistical data to develop a much more detailed and nuanced picture of slave life. Individuals were shown to have been resilient and somewhat autonomous in many of their activities, within the limits of their situation and despite its precariousness. Historians who wrote in this era include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Blassingame" title="John Blassingame">John Blassingame</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_Community" title="Slave Community">Slave Community</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Genovese" title="Eugene Genovese">Eugene Genovese</a> (<i>Roll, Jordan, Roll</i>), Leslie Howard Owens (<i>This Species of Property</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Gutman" title="Herbert Gutman">Herbert Gutman</a> (<i>The Black Family in Slavery and Freedom</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slavery_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"It is shocking to human Nature, that any Race of Mankind and their Posterity should be sentanc'd to perpetual Slavery; nor in Justice can we think otherwise of it, that they are thrown amongst us to be our Scourge one Day or other for our Sins: And as Freedom must be as dear to them as it is to us, what a Scene of Horror must it bring about! And the longer it is unexecuted, the bloody Scene must be the greater." – Inhabitants of New Inverness, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Petition_against_the_Introduction_of_Slavery" title="s:Petition against the Introduction of Slavery">s:Petition against the Introduction of Slavery</a></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-divided-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-divided_55-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://nydivided.org/VirtualExhibit/T1/G1/G1ReadMore.php" rel="nofollow">"King Cotton: Dramatic Growth of the Cotton Trade"</a>, <i>New York Divided: Slavery and the Civil War</i>, New-York Historical Society, accessed May 12, 2012</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-AFP-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-AFP_56-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-AFP_56-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.fcnl.org/issues/item.php?item_id=731&amp;issue_id=75" rel="nofollow">"Background on conflict in Liberia"</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASlavery+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.btitle=Background+on+conflict+in+Liberia&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.fcnl.org%2Fissues%2Fitem.php%3Fitem_id%3D731%26issue_id%3D75&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> <a href="/wiki/Paul_Cuffee" title="Paul Cuffee">Paul Cuffee</a>, a successful New England black shipping man, financed and captained a voyage for American blacks in 1815–1816 to British-ruled <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>. Cuffee believed that African Americans could more easily "rise to be a people" in Africa than in the U.S. because of its slavery, racial discrimination, and limits on black rights. Although Cuffee died in 1817, his early efforts encouraged the ACS to promote further settlements. The <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Friends</a> opposed slavery but believed blacks would face better chances for freedom in Africa than in the U.S. Slaveholders opposed freedom for blacks, but thought <a href="/wiki/Repatriation" title="Repatriation">repatriation</a> of freedmen was a way to remove their threat to slave societies and avoid their leading rebellions.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>The People's Chronology</i>, 1994, by James Trager.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kolchin p. 96. Through the domestic slave trade, about one million enslaved African Americans were forcibly removed from the Upper South to the Deep South, with some transported by ship in the coastwise trade. In 1834, <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> grew half the nation's cotton; by 1859, along with <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, they grew 78%. By 1859 cotton growth in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolinas" title="Carolinas">Carolinas</a> had fallen to just 10% of the national total. Berlin p. 166.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Berlin, <i>Generations of Captivity</i>, pp. 168–69. Kolchin p. 96.</span></li>

Morgan (2002). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=mhJcsiydNe8C&amp;pg=PA20" rel="nofollow"><i>Language, Discourse and Power in African American Culture</i></a>, p. 20. Cambridge University Press, 2002.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Berlin, <i>Generations of Captivity</i>, pp. 161–62.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Berlin, <i>Generations of Captivity</i>, pp. 168–69. Kolchin p. 96. Kolchin notes that Fogel and Engerman maintained that 84% of slaves moved with their families but "most other scholars assign far greater weight … to slave sales." Ransome (p. 582) notes that Fogel and Engermann based their conclusions on the study of some counties in Maryland in the 1830s and attempted to extrapolate that analysis as reflective of the entire South over the entire period.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-quote-165"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-quote_165-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://www.archives.gov/exhibits/charters/slurp_file.php?fileref=10" rel="nofollow">Charters of Freedom – The Declaration of Independence, The Constitution, The Bill of Rights</a></span>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><span class="reference-text"><b>Section 1.</b> Neither slavery nor involuntary servitude, except as a punishment for crime whereof the party shall have been duly convicted, shall exist within the United States, or any place subject to their jurisdiction. <b>Section 2.</b> Congress shall have power to enforce this article by appropriate legislation.</span></p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><span class="reference-text">— <cite><i>Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution</i></cite></span></div>
</blockquote>

<li id="cite_note-198"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-198">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A history of the descendants of the slaves of Cherokee can be found at <cite class="citation journal">Sturm, Circe (1998). "Blood Politics, Racial Classification, and Cherokee National Identity: The Trials and Tribulations of the Cherokee Freedmen". <i><a href="/wiki/American_Indian_Quarterly" title="American Indian Quarterly">American Indian Quarterly</a></i>. <b>22</b> (1/2): 230–58. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/1185118" rel="nofollow">1185118</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASlavery+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.atitle=Blood+Politics%2C+Racial+Classification%2C+and+Cherokee+National+Identity%3A+The+Trials+and+Tribulations+of+the+Cherokee+Freedmen&amp;rft.aufirst=Circe&amp;rft.aulast=Sturm&amp;rft.date=1998&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.issue=1%2F2&amp;rft.jtitle=American+Indian+Quarterly&amp;rft.pages=230-58&amp;rft.volume=22&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F1185118&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> In 1835, 7.4% of Cherokee families held slaves. In comparison, nearly one-third of white families living in Confederate states owned slaves in 1860. Further analysis of the 1835 Federal Cherokee Census can be found in <cite class="citation journal">McLoughlin, W. G.; Conser, W. H. (1977). "The Cherokees in Transition: a Statistical Analysis of the Federal Cherokee Census of 1835". <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Journal_of_American_History" title="Journal of American History">Journal of American History</a></i>. <b>64</b> (3): 678. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/1887236" rel="nofollow">1887236</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASlavery+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.atitle=The+Cherokees+in+Transition%3A+a+Statistical+Analysis+of+the+Federal+Cherokee+Census+of+1835&amp;rft.au=Conser%2C+W.+H.&amp;rft.aufirst=W.+G.&amp;rft.aulast=McLoughlin&amp;rft.date=1977&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+American+History&amp;rft.pages=678&amp;rft.volume=64&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F1887236&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> A discussion on the total number of Slave holding families can be found in <cite class="citation web">Olsen, Otto H. (December 2004). <a class="external text" href="http://www.southernhistory.net/modules.php?op=modload&amp;name=News&amp;file=article&amp;sid=9406&amp;mode=thread&amp;order=0&amp;thold=0" rel="nofollow">"Historians and the extent of slave ownership in the Southern United States"</a>. <i>Civil War History</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 8,</span> 2007</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASlavery+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.atitle=Historians+and+the+extent+of+slave+ownership+in+the+Southern+United+States&amp;rft.aufirst=Otto+H.&amp;rft.aulast=Olsen&amp;rft.date=2004-12&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Civil+War+History&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.southernhistory.net%2Fmodules.php%3Fop%3Dmodload%26name%3DNews%26file%3Darticle%26sid%3D9406%26mode%3Dthread%26order%3D0%26thold%3D0&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

Baptist">Baptist, Edward E.</a> <i>The Half Has Never Been Told: Slavery and the Making of American Capitalism.</i> <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>: <a href="/wiki/Basic_Books" title="Basic Books">Basic Books</a>, 2014. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-465-00296-X" title="Special:BookSources/0-465-00296-X">0-465-00296-X</a></li>
<li>Ryan, Tim A. <i>Calls and Responses: The American Novel of Slavery since Gone with the Wind</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baton_Rouge" title="Baton Rouge">Baton Rouge</a>: <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_State_University_Press" title="Louisiana State University Press">Louisiana State University Press</a>, 2008.</li>

Van Deburg">Van Deburg, William</a>. <i>Slavery and Race in American Popular Culture</i>. <a href="/wiki/Madison,_Wisconsin" title="Madison, Wisconsin">Madison</a>: <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin_Press" title="University of Wisconsin Press">University of Wisconsin Press</a>, 1984.</li>
</ul>
